    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="1e03b5beb6eb41eb92ffb4f20eba3320"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1e03b5beb6eb41eb92ffb4f20eba3320">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f40257c8-1571-4909-8735-f8acfdde3649'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d111728776" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>In this work, multifunctional solid nanocomposite electrolytes (SNEs) are constructed by integrating Ca-doped CeO<sub>2</sub> frameworks with fluorine-containing polymer to simultaneously address the sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> transport and the uncontrollable Na dendrite propagation for robust and high-rate all-solid-state sodium-ion batteries (ASSSIBs).
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/10e0fc89-bf23-469e-bac6-50482bc5e8b0/anie202417778-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/10e0fc89-bf23-469e-bac6-50482bc5e8b0/anie202417778-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f7271a80-094b-4985-82a8-7f698fdeb5b5/anie202417778-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/10e0fc89-bf23-469e-bac6-50482bc5e8b0/anie202417778-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d111728809" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Due to the seamless interfaces between solid polymer electrolytes (SPEs) and electrode materials, SPEs-based all-solid-state sodium-ion batteries (ASSSIBs) are considered promising energy storage systems. However, the sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> transport and uncontrollable Na dendrite propagation still hinder the practical application of SPEs-based ASSSIBs. Herein, Ca-doped CeO<sub>2</sub> (Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>) nanotube framework is synthesized and integrated with poly (ethylene oxide) methyl ether acrylate-perfluoropolyether copolymer (PEOA-PFPE), resulting in multifunctional solid nanocomposite electrolytes (namely SNEs, i.e., PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>). Our investigations demonstrate that the fluorous effect incurred by the fluorine-containing PEOA-PFPE and the oxygen vacancy effect induced by the Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> framework could synergistically promote the dissociation of sodium salt, ultimately enhancing the Na<sup>+</sup> mobility in SNEs. Besides, the resultant SNEs construct rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels and homogenize the Na deposition in SNEs/Na interface, which effectively prevents the Na dendrite growth. Furthermore, the assembled carbon-coated sodium vanadium phosphate (NVP@C)||PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>||Na coin cell delivers impressive rate capability of 97.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C and outstanding cycling stability with capacity retention of 84.3 % after 300 cycles at 1 C. This work illustrates that constructing multifunctional SNEs via incorporating functional inorganic frameworks into fluorine-containing SPEs could be a promising strategy for the commercialization of robust and high-performance ASSSIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417778-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417778-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered one of the most promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to the abundance and comparatively low cost of sodium sources.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0001_R_d111728667e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> However, the organic liquid electrolytes in conventional SIBs are flammable and can cause severe side reactions with sodium metal anode, which possibly bring about catastrophic safety issues such as combustion and explosion.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0004_R_d111728667e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> All-solid-state sodium-ion batteries (ASSSIBs) are promising candidates to relieve these safety issues by replacing conventional organic liquid electrolytes with solid electrolytes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0007_R_d111728667e577" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span> Among all solid electrolytes, solid polymer electrolytes (SPEs) possess distinct advantages owing to their excellent high flexibility and relatively low interfacial resistance, which recently have made considerable progress in improving the electrochemical performances of batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0008_R_d111728667e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a></sup></span> However, conventional poly (ethylene oxide) (PEO)-based SPEs still suffer from the low Na<sup>+</sup> transference number due to the strong coordination interactions between ethylene oxide segments and Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0009_R_d111728667e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Mater.">5</a></sup></span> Besides, SPEs-based ASSSIBs may undergo severe anionic concentration polarization in the electrode/electrolyte interface due to the parasitic electrochemical reactions between anions and Na metal anode, resulting in unsatisfactory electrochemical performances.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0010_R_d111728667e591" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Storage Mater.">6</a></sup></span> Therefore, it is crucial to develop functional SPEs with high ionic conductivity, satisfactory Na<sup>+</sup> transference number, and stable electrode/electrolyte interface for the practical ASSSIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0011_R_d111728667e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>Due to the fluorous effect, fluorine-containing organic liquid electrolytes have demonstrated promising potential in batteries for their high cationic transference number,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0012_R_d111728667e601" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">8</a></sup></span> superior interfacial stability,<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0013_R_d111728667e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">9</a></sup></span> and excellent electrochemical stability.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0014_R_d111728667e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span> Recently, fluorine-containing SPEs also displayed impressive electrochemical performances in solid-state batteries.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0015_R_d111728667e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span> For example, the interactions between fluorine-containing segments and anions of sodium salt could restrict the transport of anion groups and facilitate the dissociation of sodium salts, ultimately enhancing the ionic conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> transference number.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0008_R_d111728667e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0015_R_d111728667e616" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Commun.">11</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, the ionic conductivity and rate capability of ASSSIBs with fluorine-containing SPEs are still unsatisfactory and need to be optimized further to construct high-performance ASSSIBs.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport is the prerequisite for achieving high ionic conductivity and rate capability in ASSSIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0008_R_d111728667e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0016_R_d111728667e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12">12</a></sup></span> Therefore, the deliberate modulation of Na<sup>+</sup> transport in ASSSIBs is an effective strategy to address the above-mentioned challenges. Generally, the Na<sup>+</sup> transport in SPEs-based ASSSIBs is divided into two aspects: Na<sup>+</sup> transport in a polymeric matrix and Na<sup>+</sup> transport in electrode/electrolyte interface.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0020_R_d111728667e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> For the Na<sup>+</sup> transport in polymeric matrix, metal oxides inorganic fillers (e.g., Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, TiO<sub>2</sub>, SiO<sub>2</sub>, etc.) could be excellent platforms to decrease the crystallinity of polymers, resulting in enhanced mobility of polymeric matrix to transport Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0023_R_d111728667e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> According to the ion transport equation:<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0028_R_d111728667e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14e - Chem. Soc. Rev.">14e</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0029_R_d111728667e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Rev.">15</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="anie202417778-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f73631d3-0461-4313-ad16-c6dcb3f068ed/anie202417778-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
                  </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
            </div>
         
         <p>the charge number of the carriers (<i>q<sub>i</sub></i>), the mobility of the charged carriers (<i>u<sub>i</sub></i>), and the concentration of the charged carriers (<i>c<sub>i</sub></i>) can affect the ion transport capability in a polymeric matrix. Therefore, enhancing the concentration of movable Na<sup>+</sup> in ASSSIBs could also effectively enhance the Na<sup>+</sup> transport in the SPEs-based ASSSIBs. Furthermore, the insufficient Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels in the electrolyte/Na interface can induce significant polarization and inhomogeneous Na plating/stripping, resulting in severe Na dendrite growth and fast decay of battery performances.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0030_R_d111728667e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span> There are several strategies to facilitate the Na<sup>+</sup> transport in SPEs/Na interface in ASSSIBs, e.g., sodium-carbon (Na−C) composite engineering<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0031_R_d111728667e714" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">17</a></sup></span> and interlayer engineering.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0020_R_d111728667e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0032_R_d111728667e717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Nano Lett.">18</a></sup></span> Nevertheless, these strategies require the implementation of an additional modification process, which could increase the complexity and cost of ASSSIBs. Therefore, exploring a facile and effective strategy that can simultaneously facilitate the Na<sup>+</sup> transport in the polymeric matrix and electrolyte/electrode interface and achieve dendrite-free Na deposition electrolyte/electrode interface in SPEs-based ASSSIBs is significant and challenging.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, Ca-doped CeO<sub>2</sub> (Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>) nanotubes are synthesized and incorporated into the matrix of poly (ethylene oxide) methyl ether acrylate-perfluoropolyether copolymer (PEOA-PFPE) (Figure S1) and sodium salt (sodium bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide, NaFSI, Figure S1) to construct a multifunctional solid nanocomposite electrolyte (namely SNE, i.e., PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>), which can retain the flexible electrolyte structure to form seamless interface with Na anode, enhance the Na<sup>+</sup> transport, and inhibit the Na dendrite propagation in ASSSIBs (Scheme <a href="#anie202417778-fig-5001">1</a>). The fluorine-containing segments of the PEOA-PFPE polymer, as well as the Ca and Ce atoms (adjacent to the oxygen vacancy) on the Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> frameworks, could attract anions (i.e., FSI<sup>−</sup>), facilitate the dissociation of sodium salt (i.e., NaFSI), and offer a high concentration of movable Na<sup>+</sup> (Scheme <a href="#anie202417778-fig-5001">1</a>). In the cathode region, fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels in the electrolyte/NVP@C interface are also constructed due to the enlarged contact surface and shortened Na<sup>+</sup> transport pathway between SNE and NVP@C cathode. In the anode region, the maintained seamless contact with the sodium anode by the flexibility of the SNEs and decreased concentration polarization in the electrolyte/Na interface could significantly facilitate the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport in the SNE/Na anode interface, offer homogeneous Na deposition, and inhibit the Na dendrite propagation. Benefiting from the synergetic effects of the multi-functions for both cathode and anode, the resultant PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNE exhibits enhanced ionic conductivity, Na<sup>+</sup> transference number, and Na plating/stripping process (more than 800 h) compared to the pristine PEOA-PFPE SPE. Furthermore, the assembled NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNE deliver superior rate capability and excellent cycling stability than those with pristine PEOA-PFPE SPE. The success of this work demonstrates the promising potential of constructing multifunctional solid nanocomposite electrolytes for high-performance ASSSIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417778-fig-5001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/06f174b5-d7ca-4ddc-9fe6-8813eba6e913/anie202417778-fig-5001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/06f174b5-d7ca-4ddc-9fe6-8813eba6e913/anie202417778-fig-5001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/79d32f1e-6094-4264-9e5f-a1e448e8c64e/anie202417778-fig-5001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/06f174b5-d7ca-4ddc-9fe6-8813eba6e913/anie202417778-fig-5001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Schematic illustrations of the multi-functions of the as-prepared solid nanocomposite electrolyte (namely SNE, i.e., PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>): (a) The inherent issues of the conventional solid polymer electrolytes (SPEs): the sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> transport in the polymeric matrix and electrolyte/Na interface as well as the severe Na dendrite propagation in ASSSIBs. (b) The multifunctional SNE addresses the issues of SPEs by maintaining flexible electrolyte structure to the seamless interface with Na anode, facilitating rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport in polymeric matrix and electrolyte/Na interface as well as securing the dendrite-free Na deposition in ASSSIBs.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417778-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417778-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The Ca-doped CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes are fabricated through the introduction of Ca<sup>2+</sup> precursor into the CeO<sub>2</sub> precursor first and subsequent electrospinning as well as high-temperature calcination processes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0033_R_d111728667e803" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span> After the Ca<sup>2+</sup> doping, the Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotube (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0001">1a</a>) exhibits an identical XRD profile to the pure CeO<sub>2</sub> phase (Figure S2), indicating the same crystalline structures of CeO<sub>2</sub> before and after doping with Ca<sup>2+</sup> owing to the similar ionic radius of Ca<sup>2+</sup> (1.12 Å) and Ce<sup>4+</sup> (0.97 Å).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0033_R_d111728667e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0034_R_d111728667e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Power Sources">20</a></sup></span> Besides, the wide hump observed at the 2<i>θ</i> of 10–15 degrees is assigned to the slight formation of Ce<sup>3+</sup> because the high temperature of the calcination process in the air atmosphere could easily create oxygen vacancy, unsaturated bonds, and metal ions with different valence states.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0035_R_d111728667e832" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Sol. Energy Mater. Sol. Cells">21</a></sup></span> SEM (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0001">1b</a>–<a href="#anie202417778-fig-0001">c</a> and Figure S3a) and TEM images (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0001">1d</a> and Figure S3b) illustrate the obvious nanotube morphology with a diameter of 50–100 nm and length ranging from 400 nm to 20 μm. The crystalline structure of the Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotube is further investigated via the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0001">1e</a>) and selected area electron diffraction (SAED, Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0001">1f</a>). The clear lattice fringe with a <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.3292 nm is attributed to the (111) crystalline plane of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotube (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0001">1e</a>), which is slightly larger than that of CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotube (0.3019 nm) without Ca<sup>2+</sup> doping (Figure S4).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0033_R_d111728667e865" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span> This can be ascribed to the larger ionic radius of Ca<sup>2+</sup> than Ce<sup>4+</sup>. Furthermore, the homogeneous distribution of Ca, Ce, and O indicates that the Ca element is uniformly distributed in CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0001">1g</a>–<a href="#anie202417778-fig-0001">i</a> and Figure S5). Furthermore, the XPS results (Figure S6, Table S1–2) confirm that Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> possesses higher oxygen vacancy concentration (30.5 %) than undoped CeO<sub>2</sub> (24.8 %). Therefore, the above experimental results illustrate that Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> with a nanotube morphology structure has been synthesized successfully.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417778-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d86ca4a7-0aa1-485a-8c99-861cce184b37/anie202417778-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d86ca4a7-0aa1-485a-8c99-861cce184b37/anie202417778-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a40662d6-e38b-4093-af7b-07c50480f6c7/anie202417778-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d86ca4a7-0aa1-485a-8c99-861cce184b37/anie202417778-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417778-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417778" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Materials characterizations of the Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes: (a) XRD profile. (b–c) SEM images. (d, g) TEM images. (e) HRTEM image. (f) SAED pattern. (h, i) EDS mapping images.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>In addition, the PEOA-PFPE displays outstanding anti-flammable property because the abundant fluorine-containing segments can effectively hinder the ignition (Figure S7), illustrating the great potential of PEOA-PFPE as the polymeric matrix.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0013_R_d111728667e916" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">9</a></sup></span> Therefore, the as-prepared Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes are incorporated into the matrix of PEOA-PFPE polymer and NaFSI in acetonitrile to construct solid nanocomposite electrolytes (SNEs) with a cellulose substrate membrane through a solvent-casting method. Different amounts of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes (0, 1, 3, and 5 wt %) in the PEOA-PFPE polymeric matrix and NaFSI are named as PEOA-PFPE SPE, PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-1, PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3, PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-5 SNEs, respectively. PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE is selected as the representative of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNEs to compare electrochemical properties and battery performances with PEOA-PFPE SPE due to the highest ionic conductivity of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE among all PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNEs (which will be discussed in subsequent sections). Both PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">2a</a>) and PEOA-PFPE SPE (Figure S8a) are thin and light-yellow membranes with a thickness of around 41 μm (Figure S8b). As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure S9, the obvious XRD peaks of PEOA-PFPE polymer and PEOA-PFPE SPE at 12.5 degrees indicate that both PEOA-PFPE polymer and PEOA-PFPE SPE possess crystalline regions. In contrast, the peak at 12.5 degrees disappears in PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">2b</a>), demonstrating the decreased crystallinity of the PEOA-PFPE matrix after the incorporation of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes. The decreased crystallinity of the PEOA-PFPE matrix is beneficial for the enhancement of Na<sup>+</sup> transport.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0036_R_d111728667e957" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">22</a></sup></span> In addition, the peaks at 28.5, 33.1, and 56.4 degrees of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE are assigned to Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure S9). Furthermore, the composition and morphology of the PEOA-PFPE SPE, PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE, and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE are further confirmed by SEM and corresponding EDS mapping images. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">2c</a>–<a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">d</a> and Figure S10–12, the membranes of both PEOA-PFPE SPE, PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE, and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE are compact and full of solid electrolytes, and NaFSI is uniformly dissolved in as-fabricated solid electrolytes. In addition, the surface and cross-section of PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (Figure S11–12) and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">2c</a>–<a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">d</a>) are homogeneously distributed with CeO<sub>2</sub> and Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes, respectively. The 3D nanostructure frameworks of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNEs (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">2c</a>–<a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">d</a>) could facilitate the embedment, and even distribution of the polymer and therefore enlarge the surface contact area with the cathodic NVP@C nanoparticles. This is in strong contrast with the bulky morphology of the pristine PEOA-PFPE SPE where aggregation of nanoparticles is inevitable without this mechanism (Figure S10). Besides, the electrical conductivity of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE is 1.9×10<sup>−10</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S13), which is negligible and could not induce electronic leakage or short-circuit.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0033_R_d111728667e1012" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0037_R_d111728667e1012" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417778-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/19d985fa-eafd-4390-8f7c-7ce4f7ea0dc0/anie202417778-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/19d985fa-eafd-4390-8f7c-7ce4f7ea0dc0/anie202417778-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/341f9b88-db6d-44a2-8f63-b57fb9bbbbce/anie202417778-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/19d985fa-eafd-4390-8f7c-7ce4f7ea0dc0/anie202417778-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417778-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417778" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Materials characterization of the PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE: (a) Digital photo of the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE. (b) XRD profiles of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes, PEOA-PFPE polymer, PEOA-PFPE SPE, PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE, and NaFSI. SEM images of the surface (c) and the cross-section (d) of the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (the nanotubes are highlighted by the dashed circles). (e) TGA and (f) DSC curves of the PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The thermal properties of PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE are investigated by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA). The slight weight loss of PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">2e</a>) between 25 and 190 °C could be attributed to the loss of adsorbed gas molecules. The weight loss of PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE between 190 and 400 °C can be assigned to the decomposition of PEOA-PFPE polymer (<i>M</i><sub>n</sub>: 6400 g mol<sup>−1</sup>) and the NaFSI salt (molecular weight: 203 g mol<sup>−1</sup>) considering their low molecular weight.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0038_R_d111728667e1072" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">24</a></sup></span> The weight loss of PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE between 400 and 800 °C is a result of the decomposition of the cellulose substrate membrane. The weight of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE drops more rapid than that of PEOA-PFPE SPE between 400 and 800 °C could be assigned to the faster heat transfer in PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (nanocomposite structure) than in PEOA-PFPE SPE. The remaining weight ratio of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE is higher than that of PEOA-PFPE SPE due to the existence of stable Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes in PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE. Besides, PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNE are still in solid states and display no obvious shrinkage after the heating process at 80 °C compared to solid electrolytes before the heating process (Figure S14). Therefore, these results indicate the good thermal stability of both PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca-CeO<sub>2</sub> SNE below 200 °C. Furthermore, the glass transition temperature (<i>T</i><sub>g</sub>) is characterized by differential scanning calorimetry (DSC). Generally, solid electrolytes with lower <i>T</i><sub>g</sub> usually possess better molecular chain mobility and higher ionic conductivity.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0039_R_d111728667e1099" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span> DSC results (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">2f</a>) illustrate that the <i>T</i><sub>g</sub> of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (−37.4 °C) is lower than that of PEOA-PFPE SPE (−34.8 °C), indicating that the EO chains of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE possess better mobility than that of PEOA-PFPE SPE, which is beneficial to the Na<sup>+</sup> transport in solid electrolytes and enhancement of ionic conductivity. Furthermore, the melting point test (Figure S15) and digital photos (Figure S16) illustrate that the PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNE are still in a solid state at 80 °C due to the specific self-assembled block copolymer structure and strong fluorous effect interactions in solid electrolytes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0008_R_d111728667e1117" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>To explore the role of CeO<sub>2</sub> and Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes at an atomic scale in solid electrolytes, the DFT computation is performed to understand the interactions between CeO<sub>2</sub>/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> and NaFSI. As suggested by the XRD profile of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0001">1a</a>), the CeO<sub>2</sub> (111) surface, which corresponds to the highest XRD peak, is investigated in the theoretical simulation. The top view of the optimized atomic structures of CeO<sub>2</sub> (111) and Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> (111) are displayed in Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0003">3a</a>–<a href="#anie202417778-fig-0003">b</a>, respectively. The Ca doping leads to the formation of oxygen vacancy around the Ce atom, which strengthens the adsorption of FSI<sup>−</sup> at the oxygen vacancy site.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0040_R_d111728667e1151" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Phys. D Appl Phys.">26</a></sup></span> As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0003">3c</a> and Figure S17a, DFT results reveal that the FSI<sup>−</sup> is only coordinated with the surface Ce atom in the pristine CeO<sub>2</sub> surface. On the Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> surface, the FSI<sup>−</sup> is coordinated simultaneously with both surface Ce and Ca atoms near the oxygen vacancy site (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0003">3d</a> and Figure S17b). The low capability of PEOA-PFPE polymer to attract the FSI<sup>−</sup> has been evidenced by the small binding energy (−0.63 eV) of FSI<sup>−</sup> to PEOA-PFPE polymer (Table S3).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0008_R_d111728667e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a></sup></span> The binding energy of FSI<sup>−</sup> to the undoped CeO<sub>2</sub> surface is −1.06 eV, indicating the enhanced capability to attract the FSI<sup>−</sup> by CeO<sub>2</sub> compared to PEOA-PFPE polymer (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0003">3c</a> and Table S3). Impressively, the binding energy of FSI<sup>−</sup> to Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> surface is −2.44 eV (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0003">3c</a> and Table S3), indicating the strong capability of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> to attract the FSI<sup>−</sup>. Therefore, in pristine PEOA-PFPE SPE, most NaFSI is not completely dissociated into movable Na<sup>+</sup> to transport in the solid electrolyte, resulting in sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> transport (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0003">3e</a>). In sharp contrast, the strong binding strength between FSI<sup>−</sup> and Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> facilitates the dissociation of NaFSI to generate more movable Na<sup>+</sup>, which significantly enhances the Na<sup>+</sup> transport in PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0003">3f</a>). Furthermore, the binding energy between cation (Na<sup>+</sup>) and anion (FSI<sup>−</sup>) of NaFSI without the existence of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> is −5.31 eV. In sharp contrast, the total binding energy of Na<sup>+</sup> interacted by FSI<sup>−</sup> and Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> is −4.90 eV, indicating that NaFSI is easier to dissociate and produce movable Na<sup>+</sup> after the incorporation of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417778-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/415704b1-ec47-4741-8a3d-9c40916d7c47/anie202417778-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/415704b1-ec47-4741-8a3d-9c40916d7c47/anie202417778-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/eaf68ef3-6bec-47ac-8de3-b94735f092a0/anie202417778-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/415704b1-ec47-4741-8a3d-9c40916d7c47/anie202417778-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417778-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417778" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Theoretical computation of the binding energy between NaFSI and CeO<sub>2</sub>/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>: Top view of the optimized atomic structures of CeO<sub>2</sub> (111) (a) and Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> (111) (b). Side view of the optimized atomic structures and corresponding binding energies of FSI<sup>−</sup> adsorbed on the surface of CeO<sub>2</sub> (111) (c) and Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> (111) (d). Schematic illustration of the NaFSI salt state and Na<sup>+</sup> transport capability in PEOA-PFPE SPE (e) and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNE (f).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The attenuated total reflection Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (ATR-FTIR, Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0004">4a</a>–<a href="#anie202417778-fig-0004">b</a>, and Figure S18) is conducted to further verify the DFT calculation results about the working mechanism of CeO<sub>2</sub> and Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes in the solid nanocomposite electrolytes. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0004">4a</a>–<a href="#anie202417778-fig-0004">b</a>, the peaks at 1366.48 and 768.90 cm<sup>−1</sup> of NaFSI correspond to the stretching of -SO<sub>2</sub> and S−N−S groups, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0041_R_d111728667e1305" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">27</a></sup></span> The peak at 1238.00 cm<sup>−1</sup> of PEOA-PFPE polymer is attributed to the stretching of C−F group.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0042_R_d111728667e1310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">28</a></sup></span> After the addition of NaFSI in PEOA-PFPE polymer, the peaks of −SO<sub>2</sub>, C−F, and S−N−S groups of PEOA-PFPE polymer shift to 1360.87, 1242.08, and 742.16 cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The wavenumber shift of −SO<sub>2</sub> and S−N−S groups indicates that the chemical environment of corresponding groups is changed due to the coordination between O atoms of the PEOA-PFPE polymer and Na<sup>+</sup> of NaFSI.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0043_R_d111728667e1322" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Polymer">29</a></sup></span> The peak shift of the C−F group indicates that the NaFSI interacts with the molecular chains of PEOA-PFPE polymer due to the fluorous effects between fluorine-containing segments of PEOA-PFPE polymer and FSI<sup>−</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0008_R_d111728667e1328" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a></sup></span> After the incorporation of CeO<sub>2</sub> in PEOA-PFPE SPE, only the C−F group peak exhibits an obvious shift (1243.10 cm<sup>−1</sup>), and the peaks of −SO<sub>2</sub> and S−N−S groups display no apparent shift. This result indicates that the introduction of CeO<sub>2</sub> only affects the molecular stacking of the PEOA-PFPE polymer, but rarely influences the dissociation of NaFSI due to the relatively low binding energy between CeO<sub>2</sub> and FSI<sup>−</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0003">3</a> and Table S3). The peaks of −SO<sub>2</sub>, C−F, and S−N−S groups shift to 1362.10, 1243.11, and 742.58 cm<sup>−1</sup> after incorporating 3 wt % Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes into PEOA-PFPE SPE, respectively. This result illustrates that the introduction of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes not only affects the molecular stacking of PEOA-PFPE polymer but also highly attracts the FSI<sup>−</sup> of NaFSI, consisting well with the above-mentioned results of the decreased crystallinity of PEOA-PFPE polymer in PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0002">2b</a>) and strong binding energy between Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> and FSI<sup>−</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0003">3</a> and Table S3).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0044_R_d111728667e1371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Energy Environ. Sci.">30</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417778-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3e3276d9-b621-4276-a44d-a9f9ccff2349/anie202417778-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3e3276d9-b621-4276-a44d-a9f9ccff2349/anie202417778-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1cb5174b-7716-493f-ae65-d379d568a481/anie202417778-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3e3276d9-b621-4276-a44d-a9f9ccff2349/anie202417778-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417778-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417778" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Mechanism investigation of the ionic conductivity of the resultant solid electrolytes: The ATR-FTIR spectra of NaFSI (black line), the PEOA-PFPE polymer (red line), the PEOA-PFPE SPE (blue line), and the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (green line) in the wavenumber range of 4000–400 cm<sup>−1</sup> (a) and 1500–600 cm<sup>−1</sup> (b). Raman spectra of PEOA-PFPE SPE (c) and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (d) to determine the corresponding quantity of the FSI<sup>−</sup> with different states in solid electrolytes. (e–f) Solid-state <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectra of PEOA-PFPE SPE, PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE, and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE. The EIS of SS||SS (SS: stainless steel) coin cell with PEOA-PFPE SPE (g) and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (h) between 30 and 80 °C. (i) Ionic conductivity-temperature functions of PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNEs with different addition content of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Raman spectra are used to quantitatively determine the amount of free FSI<sup>−</sup> in solid electrolytes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0045_R_d111728667e1425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">31</a></sup></span> Generally, the FSI<sup>−</sup> in solid electrolytes is mainly composed of three states, free FSI<sup>−</sup> (i.e., dissociated FSI<sup>−</sup>), contact ion pairs (CIPs, one FSI<sup>−</sup> coordinate to one Na<sup>+</sup>), and aggregate clusters (AGGs, one FSI<sup>−</sup> coordinate to two or more Na<sup>+</sup>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0036_R_d111728667e1443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">22</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0045_R_d111728667e1443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">31</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0046_R_d111728667e1443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Electrochim. Acta">32</a></sup></span> The peaks with centers of 719, 729, and 741 cm<sup>−1</sup> are assigned to the free FSI<sup>−</sup>, CIPs, and AGGs, respectively.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0046_R_d111728667e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Electrochim. Acta">32</a></sup></span> The area percentage of the free FSI<sup>−</sup> in total FSI<sup>−</sup> (sum of the free FSI<sup>−</sup>, CIPs, and AGGs) of PEOA-PFPE SPE (10.0 %, Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0004">4c</a>) and PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (11.8 %, Figure S19) are lower than that of the free FSI<sup>−</sup> in total FSI<sup>−</sup> of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (18.4 %, Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0004">4d</a>), indicating the positive effects of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> for the dissociation of NaFSI.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0047_R_d111728667e1478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33">33</a></sup></span> The high content of dissociated FSI<sup>−</sup> in PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE also reflects the high concentration of movable Na<sup>+</sup> in solid electrolytes, which is beneficial to the enhancement of Na<sup>+</sup> transport and rate capability of ASSSIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0008_R_d111728667e1489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0050_R_d111728667e1489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - eTransportation">34</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In addition, the local chemical environment and the Na<sup>+</sup> transport mechanism in solid electrolytes are characterized by solid-state <sup>23</sup>Na nuclear magnetic resonance (<sup>23</sup>Na NMR) spectra. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0004">4e</a>, the chemical shift of PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE is equal to the chemical shift of PEOA-PFPE SPE (−17.59 ppm), indicating that the incorporation of CeO<sub>2</sub> does not influence the chemical environment of Na<sup>+</sup>. On the contrary, the chemical shift of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE upshifts to −16.88 ppm, demonstrating the higher concentration of movable Na<sup>+</sup> in PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE than PEOA-PFPE SPE.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0051_R_d111728667e1517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Energy Mater.">35</a></sup></span> Besides, two <sup>23</sup>Na NMR peaks, which represent two different chemical environments of Na<sup>+</sup>, are quantitatively investigated (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0004">4f</a>). The right one with a lower chemical shift (colored purple) represents the less movable Na<sup>+</sup> coordinated by oxygen atoms of the PEOA-PFPE polymer, while the left one with a higher chemical shift (colored orange) represents more movable Na<sup>+</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0037_R_d111728667e1532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">23</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0052_R_d111728667e1532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">36</a></sup></span> The concentration of more movable Na<sup>+</sup> in PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (53.9 %) is much higher than those in PEOA-PFPE SPE (46.0 %) and PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (46.8 %). Therefore, the above-discussed <sup>23</sup>Na NMR results also confirm that Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> can facilitate the dissociation of NaFSI and offer more movable Na<sup>+</sup>, ultimately enhancing the Na<sup>+</sup> transport in solid electrolytes.</p>
         
         <p>Furthermore, the ionic conductivities and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) profiles of PEOA-PFPE SPE, PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE, and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNEs with different addition content of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes are investigated at temperatures ranging from 30 to 80 °C (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0004">4g</a>–<a href="#anie202417778-fig-0004">i</a> and Figure S20). In particular, the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE possesses the highest ionic conductivity (0.89×10<sup>−4</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>) at 80 °C, which is higher than the PEOA-PFPE SPE (0.67×10<sup>−4</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>) and PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (0.71×10<sup>−4</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>) due to the higher concentration of movable Na<sup>+</sup> in the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE benefited from the enhanced dissociation of NaFSI by Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0053_R_d111728667e1590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">37</a></sup></span> The ionic conductivity of PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE in the solid electrolyte is desirable in the absence of a liquid solvent or plasticizer. Nevertheless, the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-5 SNE exhibits decreased ionic conductivity in comparison with PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE because the excessive Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> may form adverse aggregation and impede the continuous Na<sup>+</sup> transport in solid electrolytes.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0054_R_d111728667e1604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Lett.">38</a></sup></span> Therefore, PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE is selected to compare subsequent electrochemical properties and battery performances with PEOA-PFPE SPE, considering the highest ionic conductivity of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE among all PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNEs. In addition, PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE displays wider electrochemical stability window than PEOA-PFPE SPE (Figure S21).</p>
         
         <p>Furthermore, the Na<sup>+</sup> transference number (<i>t</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) is characterized to determine the percentage of pure Na<sup>+</sup> transport in all charge carriers of PEOA-PFPE SPE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">5a</a>), PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (Figure S22), and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">5b</a>). The higher <i>t</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (0.32) than those of PEOA-PFPE SPE (0.18) and PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (0.23) is assigned to the fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport capability in PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE and decreased transport of FSI<sup>−</sup> anions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0055_R_d111728667e1653" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Prog. Mater. Sci.">39</a></sup></span> Besides, the <i>t</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (high ratio of sodium salt and EO segment) is comparable to the reported literature (Table S4). The high <i>t</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE also indicates the decreased concentration polarization and mitigated electrochemical reactions in the electrolyte/Na interface as well as the stable Na plating/stripping process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0056_R_d111728667e1670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417778-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5e363e89-c03e-4b25-b43a-29b885c4aacb/anie202417778-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5e363e89-c03e-4b25-b43a-29b885c4aacb/anie202417778-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f98ba5e5-86ac-4aae-bca5-d519c77aec0f/anie202417778-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5e363e89-c03e-4b25-b43a-29b885c4aacb/anie202417778-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417778-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417778" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The battery performances of the Na||Na symmetrical coin cells with solid electrolytes: The chronoamperometry curves and the EIS spectra (inset) of the Na||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE (a) and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (b) before and after chronoamperometry with the constant potential of 10 mV at 80 °C. Na plating/stripping measurements of the Na||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE (c) and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (d) with the current densities of 0.02, 0.05, 0.1, and 0.2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> at 80 °C (1 h for each plating/stripping process). The SEM images of sodium metal anode in the Na||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE (e) and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (f) after 10 cycles of Na plating/stripping with the current density of 0.1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> at 80 °C (area capacity: 0.1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>). F 1s XPS spectra of the sodium metal anode (etching depth of 125 nm) in the Na||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE (g) and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (h) after 10 cycles of the Na plating/stripping with the current density of 0.1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> at 80 °C (area capacity: 0.1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>). (i) Long-term Na plating/stripping cycling stability of the Na||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE at the current density of 0.1 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> and 80 °C (area capacity: 0.1 mAh cm<sup>−2</sup>).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Subsequently, Na plating/stripping measurements are conducted to study the electrochemical stability of solid electrolytes against the Na metal anode in the Na||Na coin cells. The Na||Na coin cells with PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE are firstly investigated the rate capabilities of Na plating/stripping at the current densities of 0.02, 0.05, 0.1, and 0.2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">5c</a> and <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">d</a>). The polarization potentials of the Na||Na coin cells for both PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE grow with the increase of current densities. The Na||Na coin cells with PEOA-PFPE SPE show a sharp failure at the current density of 0.2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>. In contrast, the Na||Na coin cells with PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE achieve stable Na plating/stripping at 0.2 mA cm<sup>−2</sup>, confirming the better rate capability and lower concentration polarization of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE than PEOA-PFPE SPE owing to the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport in the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE and the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE/Na interface. The SEM images (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">5e</a> and Figure S23a–c) and corresponding EDS mapping images (Figure S23d–i) of the sodium metal anode in the Na||Na coin cell with PEOA-PFPE SPE after 10 cycles of Na plating/stripping display obvious propagation of sodium dendrites. In sharp contrast, no obvious sodium dendrite is detected on the surface of the sodium metal anode in the Na||Na coin cell with PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE after 10 cycles of Na plating/stripping (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">5f</a>), which also illustrates the more homogeneous Na plating/stripping process in the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE/Na interface compared to the PEOA-PFPE SPE/Na interface.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0057_R_d111728667e1763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a></sup></span> Subsequently, the sodium metal anodes are characterized by XPS to disclose further the mechanism of the enhanced electrochemical stability of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE. F 1s XPS spectra of the sodium metal anode in the Na||Na coin cells with PEOA-PFPE SPE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">5g</a>) and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">5h</a>) after 10 cycles exhibit dominant peaks of NaF, which are attributed to the partial electrochemical reaction between sodium metal anode and PEOA-PFPE polymer/NaFSI during Na plating/stripping process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0008_R_d111728667e1777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a></sup></span> Most of the components of SEI are formed by the reactions between NaFSI and Na anode due to the lower molecular weight of NaFSI than PEOA-PFPE polymer. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">5g</a>–<a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">h</a> and Figure S24, the intensity of NaF and N element of the SEI in the Na||Na coin cell with PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE is lower than those of the SEI in the Na||Na coin cells with PEOA-PFPE SPE, indicating the decreased amount of FSI<sup>−</sup> transport to react with Na anode in ASSSIBs with PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE than that with PEOA-PFPE SPE. This result consists well with the higher Na<sup>+</sup> transference number of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">5b</a>) compared to PEOA- PFPE SPE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">5a</a>). The decreased produced NaF could offer lower electrolyte concentration depletion, more even ion flux distribution in the electrolyte/Na interface, and consequently, the decreased growth of Na dendrite. Furthermore, Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">5i</a> illustrates the long-term cycling stability of the Na plating/stripping. The Na||Na coin cell with PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE exhibits lower polarization voltage than that with PEOA-PFPE SPE for 800 h. This result is attributed to the high Na<sup>+</sup> transference number and rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport in PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE/Na interface.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0058_R_d111728667e1818" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Small">42</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In addition, electrochemical performances of the carbon-coated sodium vanadium phosphate (NVP@C)||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE are characterized at elevated temperature (80 °C) as conventional ASSSIBs (Table S5). The CV curves of NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE possess lower voltage difference between oxidation and reduction peaks as well as higher stability than those with PEOA-PFPE SPE (Figure S25), demonstrating the enhanced electrochemical reversibility and ion transport of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE. Besides, both the NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0006">6a</a>) and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0006">6b</a>) exhibit flat and stable charge–discharge curves with obvious charge and discharge plateaus during the first five cycles at 0.1 C (1 C is equal to 117 mA g<sup>−1</sup> in this manuscript). And the NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE possess lower polarization voltage than that with the PEOA-PFPE SPE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0006">6c</a>), which also confirms the higher Na<sup>+</sup> transport capability of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE than PEOA-PFPE SPE.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0008_R_d111728667e1850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0036_R_d111728667e1850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">22</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202417778-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0266ffba-cda8-4a57-97f9-76f478428612/anie202417778-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0266ffba-cda8-4a57-97f9-76f478428612/anie202417778-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/74fa2481-1267-4730-831b-7151896db346/anie202417778-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0266ffba-cda8-4a57-97f9-76f478428612/anie202417778-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202417778-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202417778" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The battery performances of the NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE and the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE: Galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of the first five cycles of the NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE (a) and the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (b) at 0.1 C. (c) Charge-discharge curves of the first cycle of the NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE and the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE at 0.1 C. Charge-discharge curves of the NVP@C||Na coin cells with PEOA-PFPE SPE (d) and the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (e) at different current densities. (f) Rate capabilities of the NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE and the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE. (g) The comparison of rate capability of the NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE and previously reported ASSSIBs. Long-term cycling performances of the NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE and the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE at 0.2 C (h) and 1 C (i).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>As for the rate capability, the NVP@C||Na coin cell with the PEOA-PFPE SPE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0006">6d</a>–<a href="#anie202417778-fig-0006">g</a>) exhibits specific capacities of 104.0, 103.6, 102.3, 99.1, and 83.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 C, respectively. In contrast, the NVP@C||Na coin cell with the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE displays enhanced rate capability of 111.2, 108.2, 105.0, 102.4, and 97.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current density of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 C, respectively. Furthermore, the rate capability of the NVP@C|| Na coin cell with the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE is superior to most reported ASSSIBs (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0006">6g</a> and Table S6).<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0024_R_d111728667e1913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14a - ACS Energy Lett.">14a</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0059_R_d111728667e1913" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43">43</a></sup></span> The excellent rate capability of the NVP@C||Na coin cell with the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE is assigned to the fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport capability in PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE and the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE/Na interface.</p>
         
         <p>Long-term cycling performances of the NVP@C||Na coin cells are conducted to investigate the cycling stability of PEOA-PFPE SPE and PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0006">6h</a>, the NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE exhibit a higher capacity retention than that of the NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE after 50 cycles at 0.2 C. In terms of the cycling stability at 1 C, the NVP@C||Na coin cell with the PEOA-PFPE SPE (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0006">6i</a>) remains a low specific capacity of 20.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (capacity retention: 20.4 %) after 300 cycles and fluctuant Coulombic efficiency (CE) during 300 cycles, which could be attributed to the severe side reactions in the electrolyte/Na interface and growth of Na dendrite. Besides, the NVP@C||Na coin cell with the PEOA-PFPE/CeO<sub>2</sub> SNE displays enhanced cycling stability with a specific capacity of 69.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles at 1 C (Figure S26). Impressively, enhanced capacity retention of 84.3 % is achieved in the NVP@C||Na coin cell with the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE, which remains a high specific capacity of 80.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0006">6i</a>). Furthermore, the NVP@C||Na coin cell with the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE delivers an extremely high average CE of 99.6 % during 300 cycles, confirming the excellent long-term cycling stability of PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE. To disclose the mechanism of the excellent cycling stability, the EIS curves of the NVP@C||Na coin cells with the PEOA-PFPE SPE and the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE before and after cycling are investigated and illustrated in Figure S27. The interfacial resistance of NVP@C||Na coin cell with PEOA-PFPE SPE increases significantly after cycling, indicating the unstable SEI between PEOA-PFPE SPE and Na anode as well as the growth of Na dendrites. In sharp contrast, the low interfacial resistance of NVP@C||Na coin cell with PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE after cycling demonstrates that the incorporation of Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> can decrease the interfacial reactions between solid electrolyte and the Na anode as well as the growth of Na dendrites (consist well with the results in Figure <a href="#anie202417778-fig-0005">5</a> and Figure S23), form robust SEI, and maintain the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels in the robust electrolyte/electrode interface. Furthermore, the cycling stability mechanism of solid electrolytes is also revealed by the digital photos and the SEM images of the Na anode surface after cycling. The Na metal anode of the NVP@C||Na coin cell with the PEOA-PFPE SPE shows a darker surface than that of the NVP@C||Na coin cell with the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE (Figure S28) after cycling, indicating the less Na dendrites and decreased adverse electrochemical reactions and more stable SEI in PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE/Na interface compared to PEOA-PFPE SPE/Na interface. Moreover, unlike the flat and smooth surface of the pristine Na metal anode (Figure S29a–c), distinct Na dendrites propagation is detected on the Na metal anode of the NVP@C||Na coin cell with the PEOA-PFPE SPE after cycling (Figure S29d–f), representing the instability of SEI. In sharp contrast, the Na metal anode of NVP@C||Na coin cell with the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE is uniform and smooth, which has no obvious voids and Na dendrites propagation after cycling (Figure S29g–i), indicating the high stability of SEI. The above SEM results consist well with the enhanced Na plating/stripping stability, rate capability, and cycling stability of the PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE than PEOA-PFPE SPE, which could be assigned to the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport, decreased transport of FSI<sup>−</sup>, and dendrite-free Na deposition electrolyte/Na interface in ASSSIBs with multifunctional PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>-3 SNE.<span><sup><a href="#anie202417778-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0008_R_d111728667e1984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#anie202417778-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202417778-bib-0033_R_d111728667e1984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Energy Mater.">19</a></sup></span></p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202417778-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202417778-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>To enhance the rate capability and cycling stability of solid polymer electrolyte (SPE) in ASSSIBs, multifunctional solid nanocomposite electrolytes (SNEs), i.e., PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub>, are designed and synthesized by integrating PEOA-PFPE SPE with Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes. The resultant PEOA-PFPE/Ca−CeO<sub>2</sub> SNEs could enhance the dissociation of NaFSI, increase the concentration of movable Na<sup>+</sup>, construct more Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels, and shorten Na<sup>+</sup> transport pathways, resulting in much-boosted Na<sup>+</sup> transport in ASSSIBs. Furthermore, the rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport in the SNEs/Na interface benefits the homogeneous Na deposition, resulting in a dendrite-free SNEs/Na interface. As a result, the Na||Na symmetrical batteries with the resultant multifunctional SNEs achieve stable Na plating/stripping (800 h) with a low overpotential. Benefiting from the coupling effects of the multi-functions of the SNEs, including flexible electrolyte structure to form contact with Na anode, enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> transport capability in the electrolyte and electrolyte/Na interface as well as the dendrite-free Na deposition SNEs/Na interface, the as-assembled NVP@C||Na coin cells delivered an excellent rate capability of 97.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 C and outstanding cycling stability with the capacity retention of 84.3 % after 300 cycles at 1 C. This work illustrates the construction of multifunctional SNEs via integrating the oxygen vacancy effect of the functional inorganic framework with the fluorous effect of the fluorine-containing SPEs could be an excellent strategy for the commercialization of robust and high-performance ASSSIBs.</p>
         </section>
